Most notably, this would exclude the possibility that branches of philosophy like <a href="/wiki/Metaphysics" title="Metaphysics">metaphysics</a> could ever provide informative accounts of what actually exists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SEP_A_Priori_20-3"><a href="#cite_note-SEP_A_Priori-20">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SEP_Analytic_Synthetic_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-SEP_Analytic_Synthetic-65">[64]</a></sup>
</p><p>The American philosopher <a href="/wiki/Willard_Van_Orman_Quine" title="Willard Van Orman Quine">Willard Van Orman Quine</a>, in his paper "<a href="/wiki/Two_Dogmas_of_Empiricism" title="Two Dogmas of Empiricism">Two Dogmas of Empiricism</a>", famously challenged the analytic-synthetic distinction, arguing that the boundary  between the two is too blurry to provide a clear division between propositions that are true by definition and propositions that are not. While some contemporary philosophers take themselves to have offered more sustainable accounts of the distinction that are not vulnerable to Quine's objections, there is no consensus about whether or not these succeed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[65]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Science_as_knowledge_acquisition">Science as knowledge acquisition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Epistemology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Science as knowledge acquisition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_science" title="Philosophy of science">Philosophy of science</a></div>
<p>Science is often considered to be a refined, formalized, systematic, institutionalized form of the pursuit and acquisition of empirical knowledge. As such, the <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_science" title="Philosophy of science">philosophy of science</a> may be viewed variously as an application of the principles of epistemology or as a foundation for epistemological inquiry.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="The_regress_problem">The regress problem</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Epistemology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: The regress problem">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Regress_argument" title="Regress argument">Regress argument</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Regress_argument" title="Regress argument">regress problem</a> (also known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Agrippa%27s_Trilemma" title="Agrippa's Trilemma">Agrippa's Trilemma</a>) is the problem of providing a complete logical foundation for human knowledge. The traditional way of supporting a rational argument is to appeal to other rational arguments, typically using chains of reason and rules of logic. A classic example that goes back to Aristotle is deducing that <i>Socrates is mortal</i>. We have a logical rule that says <i>All humans are mortal</i> and an assertion that <i>Socrates is human</i> and we deduce that <i>Socrates is mortal</i>. In this example how do we know that Socrates is human? Presumably we apply other rules such as:  <i>All born from human females are human</i>. Which then leaves open the question how do we know that all born from humans are human? This is the regress problem: how can we eventually terminate a logical argument with some statements that do not require further justification but can still be considered rational and justified? As John Pollock stated:
</p>
<blockquote><p>... to justify a belief one must appeal to a further justified belief. This means that one of two things can be the case. Either there are some beliefs that we can be justified for holding, without being able to justify them on the basis of any other belief, or else for each justified belief there is an infinite regress of (potential) justification [the nebula theory]. On this theory there is no rock bottom of justification. Justification just meanders in and out through our network of beliefs, stopping nowhere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pollock_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pollock-67">[66]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>The apparent impossibility of completing an infinite chain of reasoning is thought by some to support <a href="/wiki/Skepticism" title="Skepticism">skepticism</a>. It is also the impetus for Descartes's famous dictum: <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/I_think,_therefore_I_am" title="I think, therefore I am">I think, therefore I am</a></i>. Descartes was looking for some logical statement that could be true without appeal to other statements.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Responses_to_the_regress_problem">Responses to the regress problem</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Epistemology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Responses to the regress problem">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Many epistemologists studying justification have attempted to argue for various types of chains of reasoning that can escape the regress problem.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Foundationalism">Foundationalism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Epistemology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Foundationalism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Foundationalism" title="Foundationalism">Foundationalists</a> respond to the regress problem by asserting that certain "foundations" or "basic beliefs" support other beliefs but do not themselves require justification from other beliefs. These beliefs might be justified because they are self-evident, infallible, or derive from reliable cognitive mechanisms. Perception, memory, and a priori intuition are often considered possible examples of basic beliefs.
</p><p>The chief criticism of foundationalism is that if a belief is not supported by other beliefs, accepting it may be arbitrary or unjustified.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[67]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Coherentism">Coherentism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Epistemology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Coherentism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Another response to the regress problem is <a href="/wiki/Coherentism" title="Coherentism">coherentism</a>, which is the rejection of the assumption that the regress proceeds according to a pattern of linear justification. To avoid the charge of circularity, <a href="/wiki/Coherentism" title="Coherentism">coherentists</a> hold that an individual belief is justified circularly by the way it fits together (coheres) with the rest of the belief system of which it is a part. This theory has the advantage of avoiding the infinite regress without claiming special, possibly arbitrary status for some particular class of beliefs. Yet, since a system can be coherent while also being wrong, coherentists face the difficulty of ensuring that the whole system <a href="/wiki/Correspondence_theory_of_truth" title="Correspondence theory of truth">corresponds</a> to reality. Additionally, most logicians agree that any argument that is circular is, at best, only trivially valid. That is, to be illuminating, arguments must operate with information from multiple premises, not simply conclude by reiterating a premise.
</p><p>Nigel Warburton writes in <i>Thinking from A to Z</i> that "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Circular_argument" title="Circular argument">[c]ircular arguments</a> are not invalid; in other words, from a logical point of view there is nothing intrinsically wrong with them. However, they are, when viciously circular, spectacularly uninformative."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[68]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Infinitism">Infinitism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Epistemology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Infinitism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>An alternative resolution to the regress problem is known as "<a href="/wiki/Infinitism" title="Infinitism">infinitism</a>". Infinitists take the infinite series to be merely potential, in the sense that an individual may have indefinitely many reasons available to them, without having consciously thought through all of these reasons when the need arises. This position is motivated in part by the desire to avoid what is seen as the arbitrariness and circularity of its chief competitors, foundationalism and coherentism. The most prominent defense of infinitism has been given by <a href="/wiki/Peter_D._Klein" title="Peter D. Klein">Peter Klein</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[69]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Foundherentism">Foundherentism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Epistemology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Foundherentism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>An intermediate position, known as "<a href="/wiki/Foundherentism" title="Foundherentism">foundherentism</a>", is advanced by <a href="/wiki/Susan_Haack" title="Susan Haack">Susan Haack</a>. Foundherentism is meant to unify foundationalism and coherentism. Haack explains the view by using a crossword puzzle as an analogy. Whereas, for example, infinitists regard the regress of reasons as taking the form of a single line that continues indefinitely, Haack has argued that chains of properly justified beliefs look more like a crossword puzzle, with various different lines mutually supporting each other.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[70]</a></sup> Thus, Haack's view leaves room for both chains of beliefs that are "vertical" (terminating in foundational beliefs) and chains that are "horizontal" (deriving their justification from coherence with beliefs that are also members of foundationalist chains of belief).
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Schools_of_thought_in_epistemology">Schools of thought in epistemology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Epistemology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Schools of thought in epistemology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Empiricism">Empiricism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Epistemology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Empiricism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:David_Hume_2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="970" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="267" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/David_Hume_2.jpg/220px-David_Hume_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/David_Hume_2.jpg/330px-David_Hume_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/David_Hume_2.jpg/440px-David_Hume_2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:David_Hume_2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/David_Hume" title="David Hume">David Hume</a>, one of the most staunch defenders of empiricism.</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Empiricism" title="Empiricism">Empiricism</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Empiricism" title="Empiricism">Empiricism</a> is a view in the theory of knowledge which focuses on the role of experience, especially experience based on <a href="/wiki/Perception" title="Perception">perceptual observations</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Sense" title="Sense">senses</a>, in the generation of knowledge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[71]</a></sup> Certain forms exempt disciplines such as <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Logic" title="Logic">logic</a> from these requirements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Uebel2015_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-Uebel2015-73">[72]</a></sup>
</p><p>There are many variants of empiricism, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/British_empiricism" title="British empiricism">British empiricism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Empiricism#Logical_empiricism" title="Empiricism">logical empiricism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Phenomenalism" title="Phenomenalism">phenomenalism</a>, and some versions of <a href="/wiki/Scottish_common_sense_realism" title="Scottish common sense realism">common sense philosophy</a>. Most forms of empiricism give epistemologically privileged status to sensory impressions or <a href="/wiki/Sense_data" title="Sense data">sense data</a>, although this plays out very differently in different cases. Some of the most famous historical empiricists include <a href="/wiki/John_Locke" title="John Locke">John Locke</a>, <a href="/wiki/David_Hume" title="David Hume">David Hume</a>, <a href="/wiki/George_Berkeley" title="George Berkeley">George Berkeley</a>, <a href="/wiki/Francis_Bacon" title="Francis Bacon">Francis Bacon</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Stuart_Mill" title="John Stuart Mill">John Stuart Mill</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rudolf_Carnap" title="Rudolf Carnap">Rudolf Carnap</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Bertrand_Russell" title="Bertrand Russell">Bertrand Russell</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rationalism">Rationalism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Epistemology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Rationalism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Rationalism" title="Rationalism">Rationalism</a></div>
<p>Rationalism is the epistemological view that reason is the chief source of knowledge and the main determinant of what constitutes knowledge. More broadly, it can also refer to any view which appeals to reason as a source of knowledge or justification. Rationalism is one of the two classical views in epistemology, the other being empiricism. Rationalists claim that the mind, through the use of reason, can directly grasp certain truths in various domains, including <a href="/wiki/Logic" title="Logic">logic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ethics" title="Ethics">ethics</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Metaphysics" title="Metaphysics">metaphysics</a>. Rationalist views can range from modest views in mathematics and logic (such as that of <a href="/wiki/Gottlob_Frege" title="Gottlob Frege">Gottlob Frege</a>) to ambitious metaphysical systems (such as that of <a href="/wiki/Baruch_Spinoza" title="Baruch Spinoza">Baruch Spinoza</a>).
</p><p>Some of the most famous rationalists include <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_Descartes" title="René Descartes">René Descartes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Baruch_Spinoza" title="Baruch Spinoza">Baruch Spinoza</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gottfried_Leibniz" title="Gottfried Leibniz">Gottfried Leibniz</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Skepticism">Skepticism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Epistemology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Skepticism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Philosophical_skepticism" title="Philosophical skepticism">Philosophical skepticism</a></div>
<p>Skepticism is a position that questions the possibility of human knowledge, either in particular domains or on a general level.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Klein_2015_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-Klein_2015-74">[73]</a></sup> Skepticism does not refer to any one specific school of philosophy, but is rather a thread that runs through many epistemological debates. <a href="/wiki/Philosophical_skepticism#Ancient_Greek_skepticism" title="Philosophical skepticism">Ancient Greek skepticism</a> began during the <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_philosophy" title="Hellenistic philosophy">Hellenistic period in philosophy</a>, which featured both <a href="/wiki/Pyrrhonism" title="Pyrrhonism">Pyrrhonism</a> (notably defended by <a href="/wiki/Pyrrho" title="Pyrrho">Pyrrho</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sextus_Empiricus" title="Sextus Empiricus">Sextus Empiricus</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Aenesidemus" title="Aenesidemus">Aenesidemus</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Academic_skepticism" title="Academic skepticism">Academic skepticism</a> (notably defended by <a href="/wiki/Arcesilaus" title="Arcesilaus">Arcesilaus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carneades" title="Carneades">Carneades</a>). Among ancient Indian philosophers, skepticism was notably defended by the <a href="/wiki/Aj%C3%B1ana" title="Ajñana">Ajñana</a> school and in the Buddhist <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Madhyamika" title="Madhyamika">Madhyamika</a> tradition. In modern philosophy, <a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_Descartes" title="René Descartes">René Descartes</a>' famous inquiry into mind and body began as an exercise in skepticism, in which he started by trying to doubt all purported cases of knowledge in order to search for something that was known with absolute <a href="/wiki/Certainty" title="Certainty">certainty</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[74]</a></sup>
</p><p>Epistemic skepticism questions whether knowledge is possible at all. Generally speaking, skeptics argue that knowledge requires <a href="/wiki/Certainty" title="Certainty">certainty</a>, and that most or all of our beliefs are <a href="/wiki/Fallibilism" title="Fallibilism">fallible</a> (meaning that our grounds for holding them always, or almost always, fall short of certainty), which would together entail that knowledge is always or almost always <a href="/wiki/Acatalepsy" title="Acatalepsy">impossible</a> for us.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SKEPTICISM_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-SKEPTICISM-76">[75]</a></sup> Characterizing knowledge as strong or weak is dependent on a person's viewpoint and their characterization of knowledge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SKEPTICISM_76-1"><a href="#cite_note-SKEPTICISM-76">[75]</a></sup> Much of modern epistemology is derived from attempts to better understand and address philosophical skepticism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Klein_2015_74-1"><a href="#cite_note-Klein_2015-74">[73]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Pyrrhonism">Pyrrhonism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Epistemology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Pyrrhonism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Pyrrhonism" title="Pyrrhonism">Pyrrhonism</a></div>
<p>One of the oldest forms of epistemic skepticism can be found in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Agrippa%27s_trilemma" title="Agrippa's trilemma">Agrippa's trilemma</a> (named after the <a href="/wiki/Pyrrhonism" title="Pyrrhonism">Pyrrhonist</a> philosopher <a href="/wiki/Agrippa_the_Skeptic" title="Agrippa the Skeptic">Agrippa the Skeptic</a>) which demonstrates that certainty can not be achieved with regard to beliefs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AncientSkepticism_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-AncientSkepticism-77">[76]</a></sup> Pyrrhonism dates back to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pyrrho_of_Elis" title="Pyrrho of Elis">Pyrrho of Elis</a> from the 4th century BCE, although most of what we know about Pyrrhonism today is from the surviving works of <a href="/wiki/Sextus_Empiricus" title="Sextus Empiricus">Sextus Empiricus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AncientSkepticism_77-1"><a href="#cite_note-AncientSkepticism-77">[76]</a></sup> Pyrrhonists claim that for any argument for a non-evident proposition, an equally convincing argument for a contradictory proposition can be produced. Pyrrhonists do not dogmatically deny the possibility of knowledge, but instead point out that beliefs about non-evident matters cannot be substantiated.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Cartesian_skepticism">Cartesian skepticism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Epistemology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Cartesian skepticism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Evil_demon" title="Evil demon">Cartesian evil demon problem</a>, first raised by <a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_Descartes" title="René Descartes">René Descartes</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[note 2]</a></sup> supposes that our sensory impressions may be controlled by some external power rather than the result of ordinary veridical perception.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Descartes1_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-Descartes1-79">[77]</a></sup> In such a scenario, nothing we sense would actually exist, but would instead be mere illusion. As a result, we would never be able to know anything about the world, since we would be systematically deceived about everything. The conclusion often drawn from evil demon skepticism is that even if we are not completely deceived, all of the information provided by our senses is still <i>compatible</i> with skeptical scenarios in which we are completely deceived, and that we must therefore either be able to exclude the possibility of deception or else must deny the possibility of <i>infallible</i> knowledge (that is, knowledge which is completely certain) beyond our immediate sensory impressions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Descartes2_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-Descartes2-80">[78]</a></sup> While the view that no beliefs are beyond doubt other than our immediate sensory impressions is often ascribed to Descartes, he in fact thought that we <i>can</i> exclude the possibility that we are systematically deceived, although his reasons for thinking this are based on a highly contentious <a href="/wiki/Ontological_argument" title="Ontological argument">ontological argument</a> for the existence of a benevolent God who would not allow such deception to occur.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Descartes1_79-1"><a href="#cite_note-Descartes1-79">[77]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Responses_to_philosophical_skepticism">Responses to philosophical skepticism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Epistemology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Responses to philosophical skepticism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Epistemological skepticism can be classified as either "mitigated" or "unmitigated" skepticism. Mitigated skepticism rejects "strong" or "strict" knowledge claims but does approve weaker ones, which can be considered "virtual knowledge", but only with regard to justified beliefs.  Unmitigated skepticism rejects claims of both virtual and strong knowledge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SKEPTICISM_76-2"><a href="#cite_note-SKEPTICISM-76">[75]</a></sup> Characterizing knowledge as strong, weak, virtual or genuine can be determined differently depending on a person's viewpoint as well as their characterization of knowledge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SKEPTICISM_76-3"><a href="#cite_note-SKEPTICISM-76">[75]</a></sup> Some of the most notable attempts to respond to unmitigated skepticism include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Direct_realism" title="Direct realism">direct realism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Disjunctivism" title="Disjunctivism">disjunctivism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Scottish_common_sense_realism" title="Scottish common sense realism">common sense philosophy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pragmatism" title="Pragmatism">pragmatism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fideism" title="Fideism">fideism</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Fictionalism" title="Fictionalism">fictionalism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[79]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pragmatism">Pragmatism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Epistemology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Pragmatism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Pragmatism" title="Pragmatism">Pragmatism</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Pragmatism" title="Pragmatism">Pragmatism</a> is a fallibilist epistemology that emphasizes the role of action in knowing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PragmatismSEP_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-PragmatismSEP-82">[80]</a></sup> Different interpretations of pragmatism variously emphasize: truth as the final outcome of ideal scientific inquiry and experimentation, truth as closely related to usefulness, experience as transacting with instead of representing nature, and human practices as the foundation of language.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PragmatismSEP_82-1"><a href="#cite_note-PragmatismSEP-82">[80]</a></sup> Pragmatism's origins are often attributed to <a href="/wiki/Charles_Sanders_Peirce" title="Charles Sanders Peirce">Charles Sanders Peirce</a>, <a href="/wiki/William_James" title="William James">William James</a>, and <a href="/wiki/John_Dewey" title="John Dewey">John Dewey</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PragmatismSEP_82-2"><a href="#cite_note-PragmatismSEP-82">[80]</a></sup> In 1878, Peirce formulated the maxim: "Consider what effects, that might conceivably have practical bearings, we conceive the object of our conception to have. Then, our conception of these effects is the whole of our conception of the object."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[81]</a></sup>
</p><p>William James suggested that through a pragmatist epistemology, theories "become instruments, not answers to enigmas in which we can rest".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PragmatismJames_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-PragmatismJames-84">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 28">: 28 </span></sup> In James's pragmatic method, which he adapted from Peirce, metaphysical disputes can be settled by tracing the practical consequences of the different sides of the argument. If this process does not resolve the dispute, then "the dispute is idle".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PragmatismJames_84-1"><a href="#cite_note-PragmatismJames-84">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 25">: 25 </span></sup>
</p><p>Contemporary versions of pragmatism have been developed by thinkers such as <a href="/wiki/Richard_Rorty" title="Richard Rorty">Richard Rorty</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hilary_Putnam" title="Hilary Putnam">Hilary Putnam</a>. Rorty proposed that values were historically contingent and dependent upon their utility within a given historical period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[83]</a></sup> Contemporary philosophers working in pragmatism are called <a href="/wiki/Neopragmatism" title="Neopragmatism">neopragmatists</a>, and also include <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_Rescher" title="Nicholas Rescher">Nicholas Rescher</a>, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Brandom" title="Robert Brandom">Robert Brandom</a>, <a href="/wiki/Susan_Haack" title="Susan Haack">Susan Haack</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cornel_West" title="Cornel West">Cornel West</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Naturalized_epistemology">Naturalized epistemology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Epistemology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Naturalized epistemology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Naturalized_epistemology" title="Naturalized epistemology">Naturalized epistemology</a></div>
<p>In certain respects an intellectual descendant of pragmatism, <a href="/wiki/Naturalized_epistemology" title="Naturalized epistemology">naturalized epistemology</a> considers the evolutionary role of knowledge for agents living and evolving in the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Epistemology_Naturalized_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-Epistemology_Naturalized-86">[84]</a></sup> It de-emphasizes the questions around justification and truth, and instead asks, empirically, how reliable beliefs are formed and the role that evolution played in the development of such processes. It suggests a more empirical approach to the subject as a whole, leaving behind philosophical definitions and consistency arguments, and instead using psychological methods to study and understand how "knowledge" is actually formed and is used in the natural world. As such, it does not attempt to answer the analytic questions of traditional epistemology, but rather replace them with new empirical ones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kim_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kim-87">[85]</a></sup>
</p><p>Naturalized epistemology was first proposed in "Epistemology Naturalized", a seminal paper by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/W.V.O._Quine" title="W.V.O. Quine">W.V.O. Quine</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Epistemology_Naturalized_86-1"><a href="#cite_note-Epistemology_Naturalized-86">[84]</a></sup> A less radical view has been defended by <a href="/wiki/Hilary_Kornblith" title="Hilary Kornblith">Hilary Kornblith</a> in <i>Knowledge and its Place in Nature</i>, in which he seeks to turn epistemology towards empirical investigation without completely abandoning traditional epistemic concepts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kornblith_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kornblith-88">[86]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Epistemic_relativism">Epistemic relativism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Epistemology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Epistemic relativism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Relativism" title="Relativism">Relativism</a></div>
<p>Epistemic relativism is the view that what is true, rational, or justified for one person need not be true, rational, or justified for another person. Epistemic relativists therefore assert that while there are <i>relative</i> facts about truth, rationality, justification, and so on, there is no <i>perspective-independent</i> fact of the matter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boghossian_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-Boghossian-89">[87]</a></sup> Note that this is distinct from epistemic <a href="/wiki/Contextualism" title="Contextualism">contextualism</a>, which holds that the <i>meaning</i> of epistemic terms vary across contexts (e.g. "I know" might mean something different in everyday contexts and skeptical contexts). In contrast, epistemic relativism holds that the relevant <i>facts</i> vary, not just linguistic meaning. Relativism about truth may also be a form of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ontological" title="Ontological">ontological</a> relativism, insofar as relativists about truth hold that facts about what <i>exists</i> vary based on perspective.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boghossian_89-1"><a href="#cite_note-Boghossian-89">[87]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Epistemic_constructivism">Epistemic constructivism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Epistemology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Epistemic constructivism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Social_constructivism" title="Social constructivism">Social constructivism</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Social_constructivism" title="Social constructivism">Constructivism</a> is a view in philosophy according to which all "knowledge is a compilation of human-made constructions",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[88]</a></sup> "not the neutral discovery of an objective truth".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[89]</a></sup> Whereas objectivism is concerned with the "object of our knowledge", constructivism emphasizes "how we construct knowledge".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[90]</a></sup> Constructivism proposes new definitions for <a href="/wiki/Knowledge" title="Knowledge">knowledge</a> and <a href="/wiki/Truth" title="Truth">truth</a>, which emphasize intersubjectivity rather than objectivity, and viability rather than truth. The constructivist point of view is in many ways comparable to certain forms of pragmatism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[91]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Epistemic_idealism">Epistemic idealism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Epistemology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Epistemic idealism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Idealism" title="Idealism">Idealism</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Idealism" title="Idealism">Idealism</a> is a broad term referring to both an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ontological" title="Ontological">ontological</a> view about the world being in some sense mind-dependent and a corresponding epistemological view that everything we know can be reduced to mental phenomena. First and foremost, "idealism" is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metaphysical" title="Metaphysical">metaphysical</a> doctrine. As an epistemological doctrine, idealism shares a great deal with both empiricism and rationalism. Some of the most famous empiricists have been classified as idealists (particularly <a href="/wiki/George_Berkeley" title="George Berkeley">Berkeley</a>), and yet the <a href="/wiki/Subjectivism" title="Subjectivism">subjectivism</a> inherent to idealism also resembles that of <a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_Descartes" title="René Descartes">Descartes</a> in many respects. Many idealists believe that knowledge is primarily (at least in some areas) acquired by <i>a priori</i> processes, or that it is <a href="/wiki/Innatism" title="Innatism">innate</a>—for example, in the form of concepts not derived from experience.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[92]</a></sup> The relevant theoretical concepts may purportedly be part of the structure of the human <a href="/wiki/Mind" title="Mind">mind</a> (as in <a href="/wiki/Immanuel_Kant" title="Immanuel Kant">Kant</a>'s theory of <a href="/wiki/Transcendental_idealism" title="Transcendental idealism">transcendental idealism</a>), or they may be said to exist independently of the mind (as in Plato's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Theory_of_Forms" title="Theory of Forms">theory of Forms</a>).
</p><p>Some of the most famous forms of idealism include <a href="/wiki/Transcendental_idealism" title="Transcendental idealism">transcendental idealism</a> (developed by <a href="/wiki/Immanuel_Kant" title="Immanuel Kant">Immanuel Kant</a>), <a href="/wiki/Subjective_idealism" title="Subjective idealism">subjective idealism</a> (developed by <a href="/wiki/George_Berkeley" title="George Berkeley">George Berkeley</a>), and <a href="/wiki/Absolute_idealism" title="Absolute idealism">absolute idealism</a> (developed by <a href="/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel" title="Georg Wilhelm Friedrich Hegel">Georg Wilhelm Friedrich Hegel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Wilhelm_Joseph_Schelling" title="Friedrich Wilhelm Joseph Schelling">Friedrich Schelling</a>).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bayesian_epistemology">Bayesian epistemology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Epistemology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Bayesian epistemology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Bayesian_epistemology" title="Bayesian epistemology">Bayesian epistemology</a> is a formal approach to various topics in epistemology that has its roots in <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Bayes" title="Thomas Bayes">Thomas Bayes</a>' work in the field of probability theory. One advantage of its formal method in contrast to traditional epistemology is that its concepts and theorems can be defined with a high degree of precision. It is based on the idea that beliefs can be interpreted as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Subjective_probability" title="Subjective probability">subjective probabilities</a>. As such, they are subject to the laws of <a href="/wiki/Probability_theory" title="Probability theory">probability theory</a>, which act as the norms of <a href="/wiki/Rationality" title="Rationality">rationality</a>. These norms can be divided into static constraints, governing the rationality of beliefs at any moment, and dynamic constraints, governing how rational agents should change their beliefs upon receiving new evidence. The most characteristic Bayesian expression of these principles is found in the form of <a href="/wiki/Dutch_book" title="Dutch book">Dutch books</a>, which illustrate irrationality in agents through a series of bets that lead to a loss for the agent no matter which of the probabilistic events occurs. Bayesians have applied these fundamental principles to various epistemological topics but Bayesianism does not cover all topics of traditional epistemology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Talbott_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-Talbott-95">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[95]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[96]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Feminist_epistemology">Feminist epistemology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Epistemology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Feminist epistemology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Feminist_epistemology" title="Feminist epistemology">Feminist epistemology</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Feminist_epistemology" title="Feminist epistemology">Feminist epistemology</a> is a subfield of epistemology which applies <a href="/wiki/Feminist_theory" title="Feminist theory">feminist theory</a> to epistemological questions. It began to emerge as a distinct subfield in the 20th century. Prominent feminist epistemologists include <a href="/wiki/Miranda_Fricker" title="Miranda Fricker">Miranda Fricker</a> (who developed the concept of <a href="/wiki/Epistemic_injustice" title="Epistemic injustice">epistemic injustice</a>), <a href="/wiki/Donna_Haraway" title="Donna Haraway">Donna Haraway</a> (who first proposed the concept of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Situated_knowledge" title="Situated knowledge">situated knowledge</a>), <a href="/wiki/Sandra_Harding" title="Sandra Harding">Sandra Harding</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_S._Anderson" title="Elizabeth S. Anderson">Elizabeth Anderson</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SEP_Feminist_Epistemology_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-SEP_Feminist_Epistemology-99">[97]</a></sup> Harding proposes that feminist epistemology can be broken into three distinct categories: Feminist empiricism, standpoint epistemology, and postmodern epistemology.
</p><p>Feminist epistemology has also played a significant role in the development of many debates in <a href="/wiki/Social_epistemology" title="Social epistemology">social epistemology</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SEP_Feminist_Social_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-SEP_Feminist_Social-100">[98]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Decolonial_epistemology">Decolonial epistemology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Epistemology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Decolonial epistemology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Decolonization_of_knowledge" title="Decolonization of knowledge">Decolonization of knowledge</a></div>
<p>Epistemicide<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[99]</a></sup> is a term used in decolonisation studies that describes the killing of knowledge systems under systemic oppression such as colonisation and slavery. The term was coined by <a href="/wiki/Boaventura_de_Sousa_Santos" title="Boaventura de Sousa Santos">Boaventura de Sousa Santos</a>, who presented the significance of such physical violence creating the centering of Western knowledge in the current world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[100]</a></sup> This term challenges the thought of what is seen as knowledge in academia today.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Indian_pramana">Indian pramana</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Epistemology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Indian pramana">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Pramana" title="Pramana">Pramana</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Indian_philosophy" title="Indian philosophy">Indian schools of philosophy</a>, such as the <a href="/wiki/Hindu_philosophy" title="Hindu philosophy">Hindu</a> <a href="/wiki/Nyaya" title="Nyaya">Nyaya</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carvaka" title="Carvaka">Carvaka</a> schools, and the <a href="/wiki/Jain_philosophy" title="Jain philosophy">Jain</a> and <a href="/wiki/Buddhist_philosophy" title="Buddhist philosophy">Buddhist</a> philosophical schools, developed an epistemological tradition independently of the Western philosophical tradition called "pramana". Pramana can be translated as "instrument of knowledge" and refers to various means or sources of knowledge that Indian philosophers held to be reliable. Each school of Indian philosophy had their own theories about which pramanas were valid means to knowledge and which were unreliable (and why).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[101]</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Vedas" title="Vedas">Vedic</a> text, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Taittiriya_Aranyaka" title="Taittiriya Aranyaka">Taittirīya Āraṇyaka</a> (c. 9th–6th centuries BCE), lists "four means of attaining correct knowledge": <i>smṛti</i> ("tradition" or "scripture"), <i>pratyakṣa</i> ("perception"), <i>aitihya</i> ("communication by one who is expert", or "tradition"), and <i>anumāna</i> ("reasoning" or "inference").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[103]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the Indian traditions, the most widely discussed pramanas are: <i>Pratyakṣa</i> (perception), <i>Anumāṇa</i> (inference), <i>Upamāṇa</i> (comparison and analogy), <i>Arthāpatti</i> (postulation, derivation from circumstances), <i>Anupalabdi</i> (non-perception, negative/cognitive proof) and <i>Śabda</i> (word, testimony of past or present reliable experts). While the Nyaya school (beginning with the <a href="/wiki/Ny%C4%81ya_S%C5%ABtras" title="Nyāya Sūtras">Nyāya Sūtras</a> of Gotama, between 6th-century BCE and 2nd-century CE<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[105]</a></sup>) were a proponent of realism and supported four pramanas (perception, inference, comparison/analogy and testimony), the Buddhist epistemologists (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dignaga" title="Dignaga">Dignaga</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dharmakirti" title="Dharmakirti">Dharmakirti</a>) generally accepted only perception and inference. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carvaka" title="Carvaka">Carvaka</a> school of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Materialists" title="Materialists">materialists</a> only accepted the pramana of perception, and hence were among the first <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Empiricists" title="Empiricists">empiricists</a> in the Indian traditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[106]</a></sup> Another school, the <a href="/wiki/Aj%C3%B1ana" title="Ajñana">Ajñana</a>, included notable proponents of <a href="/wiki/Philosophical_skepticism" title="Philosophical skepticism">philosophical skepticism</a>.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Buddhist_philosophy#Epistemology" title="Buddhist philosophy">theory of knowledge of the Buddha</a> in the early Buddhist texts has been interpreted as a form of pragmatism as well as a form of correspondence theory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[107]</a></sup>  Likewise, the Buddhist philosopher <a href="/wiki/Dharmakirti" title="Dharmakirti">Dharmakirti</a> has been interpreted both as holding a form of pragmatism or correspondence theory for his view that what is true is what has effective power (<i>arthakriya</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[109]</a></sup> The Buddhist <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Madhyamika" title="Madhyamika">Madhyamika</a> school's theory of emptiness (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shunyata" title="Shunyata">shunyata</a>) meanwhile has been interpreted as a form of <a href="/wiki/Philosophical_skepticism" title="Philosophical skepticism">philosophical skepticism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[110]</a></sup>
</p><p>The main contribution to epistemology by the Jains has been their theory of "many sided-ness" or "multi-perspectivism" (<a href="/wiki/Anekantavada" title="Anekantavada">Anekantavada</a>), which says that since the world is multifaceted, any single viewpoint is limited (<i>naya</i> – a partial standpoint).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[111]</a></sup> This has been interpreted as a kind of pluralism or <a href="/wiki/Perspectivism" title="Perspectivism">perspectivism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[112]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[113]</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Jain_epistemology" title="Jain epistemology">Jain epistemology</a>, none of the pramanas gives absolute or perfect knowledge since they are each limited points of view.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Domains_of_inquiry_in_epistemology">Domains of inquiry in epistemology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Epistemology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: Domains of inquiry in epistemology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Social_epistemology">Social epistemology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Epistemology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=46" title="Edit section: Social epistemology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Social_epistemology" title="Social epistemology">Social epistemology</a></div>
<p>Social epistemology deals with questions about knowledge in contexts where our knowledge attributions cannot be explained by simply examining individuals in isolation from one another, meaning that the scope of our knowledge attributions must be widened to include broader social contexts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SEP_social_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-SEP_social-116">[114]</a></sup> It also explores the ways in which interpersonal beliefs can be justified in social contexts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SEP_social_116-1"><a href="#cite_note-SEP_social-116">[114]</a></sup> The most common topics discussed in contemporary social epistemology are <a href="/wiki/Testimony" title="Testimony">testimony</a>, which deals with the conditions under which a belief "x is true" which resulted from being told "x is true" constitutes knowledge; peer disagreement, which deals with when and how I should revise my beliefs in light of other people holding beliefs that contradict mine; and group epistemology, which deals with what it means to attribute knowledge to groups rather than individuals, and when group knowledge attributions are appropriate.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Formal_epistemology">Formal epistemology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Epistemology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=47" title="Edit section: Formal epistemology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Formal_epistemology" title="Formal epistemology">Formal epistemology</a></div>
<p>Formal epistemology uses formal tools and methods from <a href="/wiki/Decision_theory" title="Decision theory">decision theory</a>, <a href="/wiki/Logic" title="Logic">logic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Probability_theory" title="Probability theory">probability theory</a> and <a href="/wiki/Computability_theory" title="Computability theory">computability theory</a> to model and reason about issues of epistemological interest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SEP_Formal_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-SEP_Formal-117">[115]</a></sup> Work in this area spans several academic fields, including <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Computer_science" title="Computer science">computer science</a>, <a href="/wiki/Economics" title="Economics">economics</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Statistics" title="Statistics">statistics</a>. The focus of formal epistemology has tended to differ somewhat from that of traditional epistemology, with topics like uncertainty, induction, and belief revision garnering more attention than the analysis of knowledge, skepticism, and issues with justification.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Metaepistemology">Metaepistemology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Epistemology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=48" title="Edit section: Metaepistemology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Metaepistemology" title="Metaepistemology">Metaepistemology</a></div>
<p>Metaepistemology is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metaphilosophical" title="Metaphilosophical">metaphilosophical</a> study of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Philosophical_method" title="Philosophical method">methods</a>, aims, and subject matter of epistemology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[116]</a></sup> In general, metaepistemology aims to better understand our first-order epistemological inquiry. Some goals of metaepistemology are identifying inaccurate assumptions made in epistemological debates and determining whether the questions asked in mainline epistemology are the <i>right</i> epistemological questions to be asking.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Epistemology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=49" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Epistemology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=50" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Epistemology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=51" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1011085734">.mw-parser-output .reflist{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em;list-style-type:decimal}.mw-parser-output .reflist .references{font-size:100%;margin-bottom:0;list-style-type:inherit}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-2{column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-3{column-width:25em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns{margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns ol{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns li{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-alpha{list-style-type:upper-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-roman{list-style-type:upper-roman}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-alpha{list-style-type:lower-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-greek{list-style-type:lower-greek}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-roman{list-style-type:lower-roman}</style><div class="reflist">
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-19"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-19">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In Scots, the distinction is between <span title="Scots-language text"><i lang="sco">wit</i></span> and <span title="Scots-language text"><i lang="sco"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/ken#Scots" title="wikt:ken">ken</a></i></span>. In French, Portuguese, Spanish, Romanian, German, and Dutch 'to know (a person)' is translated using <span title="French-language text"><i lang="fr">connaître</i></span>, <span title="Portuguese-language text"><i lang="pt">conhecer</i></span>, <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">conocer</i></span>, <span title="Romanian-language text"><i lang="ro">a cunoaște</i></span> and <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">kennen</i></span> (both German and Dutch) respectively, whereas 'to know (how to do something)' is translated using <span title="French-language text"><i lang="fr">savoir</i></span>, <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">saber</i></span> (both Portuguese and Spanish), <span title="Romanian-language text"><i lang="ro">a şti</i></span>, <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">wissen</i></span>, and <span title="Dutch-language text"><i lang="nl">weten</i></span>. Modern Greek has the verbs <span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el">γνωρίζω</span></span> (<span title="Greek-language text"><i lang="el-Latn">gnorízo</i></span>) and <span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el">ξέρω</span></span> (<span title="Greek-language text"><i lang="el-Latn">kséro</i></span>). Italian has the verbs <span title="Italian-language text"><i lang="it">conoscere</i></span> and <span title="Italian-language text"><i lang="it">sapere</i></span> and the nouns for 'knowledge' are <span title="Italian-language text"><i lang="it">conoscenza</i></span> and <span title="Italian-language text"><i lang="it">sapienza</i></span>. German has the verbs <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">wissen</i></span> and <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">kennen</i></span>; the former implies knowing a fact, the latter knowing in the sense of being acquainted with and having a working knowledge of; there is also a noun derived from <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">kennen</i></span>, namely <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">Erkennen</i></span>, which has been said to imply knowledge in the form of recognition or acknowledgment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> The verb itself implies a process: you have to go from one state to another, from a state of "not-<span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">erkennen</i></span>" to a state of true <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">erkennen</i></span>. This verb seems the most appropriate in terms of describing the "episteme" in one of the modern European languages, hence the German name "<span title="German-language text"><i lang="de"><a class="extiw" href="https://de.wikipedia.org/wiki/Erkenntnistheorie" title="de:Erkenntnistheorie">Erkenntnistheorie</a></i></span>".</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-78"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-78">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Skeptical_scenario" title="Skeptical scenario">Skeptical scenarios</a> in a similar vein date back to <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a>'s <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Allegory_of_the_Cave" title="Allegory of the Cave">Allegory of the Cave</a></i>, although Plato's Allegory was quite different in both presentation and interpretation. In contemporary philosophical literature, something akin to evil demon skepticism is presented in <a href="/wiki/Brain_in_a_vat" title="Brain in a vat">brain in a vat</a> scenarios. See also <a class="external text" href="https://www.iep.utm.edu/evil-new/" rel="nofollow">the New Evil Demon Problem (IEP)</a>.</span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>
